The 'Ashir's Credibility: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on the 'Ashir's credibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the 'Ashir is not believed in his claim of taking 'ushr and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Hanafi analogy.

Core Idea
The 'Ashir's claim is not accepted; public treasury rights cannot be invalidated by a single person's word.

This lesson delves into an inter-madhhab dispute about the credibility of the 'Ashir (tax collector). The Hanafi school holds that the 'Ashir is not believed when he claims to have taken the 'ushr from a merchant, requiring the merchant to pay again. The Shafi'i school disagrees. We will examine the proofs and the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly state that the 'Ashir is not believed in his claim of having taken 'ushr; the 'ushr is taken again from the merchant, except for the case of umm walad.
This is the default rule.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that the 'Ashir is believed because he delivered the right to its rightful owner, thus the merchant's obligation is fulfilled.
Understand the reasoning: delivery validates the claim.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is based on analogy: like jizya and debt paid to a minor's guardian, the authoritative taking cannot be invalidated by the taker's claim. The 'Ashir is an agent of the ruler; his claim is against the public treasury.
The key is the nature of the right: it belongs to the treasury, not the 'Ashir personally.
4
Contrast the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the 'Ashir's claim is self-serving and cannot override the treasury's right, unlike a delivery to a rightful owner which is a private matter.
The difference hinges on whether the 'Ashir acts as a witness or as an adversary.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under the Hanafi school, the merchant must pay again unless he has proof; under the Shafi'i school, the merchant is freed by the 'Ashir's word.
This affects merchants crossing frontiers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العاشر
إلا في السوائم في دفعه بنفسه، وفيما صدّق المسلم صدق الذمي لا الحربي، إلا في
أم ولده. [في دفعه بنفسه: أي لا يصدق في هذه الدعوى وإن حلف، بل تؤخذ منه ثانياً ويصدق في باقي الصور. (ط،ع)] وقال الشافعي: يصدق؛ لأنه أوصل الحق إلى مستحقه، ولنا أن أخذ الحق للسلاطين في الأموال الظاهرة، فلا يمكن إبطاله كالجزية والدين للصغير إذا دفع إليه المديون، فإن للولي أن يأخذه ثانياً. (مستخلص) صدق الذمي: لأنه في دارنا كالمسلم في المعاملات وأحكامها، إلا أنه لا يصدق في الجزية إذا قال: أنا أديتها. (ع)] لأن ما يؤخذ منهم ضعف ما يؤخذ من المسلمين، فيراعى فيه شرائطه تحقيقاً للتضعيف، إلا إذا قال: "أديتها إلى فقراء أهل الذمة في المصر"، فإنه لا يصدق؛ لأن المأخوذ جزية، وليسوا بمصارف لها، ولو صرفها إلى مصالح المسلمين فليس له ولاية ذلك. (فتح) أم ولده بأن كانت معه جارية، فقال: هذه أم ولدي، فإنه يصدق؛ لأن إقراره بنسب من في يده منه صحيح، وكونه حربياً لا ينافي الاستيلاد. [رمز الحقائق: ١٢٥/١]
Chapter of the 'Ashir. Except in the case of grazing livestock when he pays it himself, and in what the Muslim is believed, the dhimmi is believed, not the harbi, except in the case of his umm walad. [In paying it himself: meaning he is not believed in this claim even if he swears, rather it is taken from him a second time, and he is believed in the remaining cases. (T, A)] And al-Shafi'i said: He is believed because he delivered the right to its claimant. And for us: The taking of rights by rulers concerning apparent wealth cannot be invalidated, like the jizya and the debt of a minor when paid to him, the guardian can take it a second time. (Mukhtasar) The dhimmi is believed because he is in our abode like a Muslim in transactions and their rulings, except he is not believed concerning jizya if he says: I have paid it. (A)] Because what is taken from them is double what is taken from Muslims, so its conditions are observed to actualize the doubling, unless he says: 'I paid it to the poor of the ahl al-dhimma in the city,' then he is not believed because what is taken is jizya, and they are not recipients of it. And if he spent it on the interests of Muslims, he has no authority for that. (Fath) The umm walad: if he had a slave-girl and said: This is my umm walad, he is believed because his acknowledgment of lineage of one in his possession is valid, and being a harbi does not conflict with istilad. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/125]
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the credibility of the 'Ashir (tax collector) when he claims to have taken the 'ushr (tithe) from a merchant passing the frontier. The Hanafi position is that the 'Ashir is not believed in his claim, and the 'ushr is taken from the merchant a second time, except in the case of a claim regarding an umm walad (a slave-girl who has borne her master a child). The core reasoning is that the 'Ashir acts as an agent of the ruler, and his taking is an authoritative act; invalidating it would lead to loss of public funds, analogous to jizya or a debt paid to a minor's guardian where the guardian can take it again. Al-Shafi'i disagreed, arguing that the 'Ashir delivered the right to its owner. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the 'Ashir's claim is against the interest of the public treasury, so he is not trusted. For the dhimmi, he is believed in normal transactions but not in claiming jizya payment because the jizya is a fixed obligation on him and his claim would nullify the ruler's right. The exception of am walad is because the acknowledgment of lineage from a man to a child in his possession is valid even if he is a harbi, as it relates to personal status.

Key Terms
العاشر (al-'āshir): The tax collector at the frontier who collects 'ushr (tithe) on goods entering the Muslim territory.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child from her master, the child is free and she becomes free upon the master's death.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under protection in an Islamic state, paying jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the analogy used to argue that the 'Ashir is not believed?
A) Analogy to a husband claiming divorce
B) Analogy to jizya and debt to a minor
C) Analogy to a thief returning stolen goods
D) Analogy to a witness in court

Key Takeaway
The 'Ashir's claim of taking 'ushr is not accepted; the merchant must pay again unless there is proof, because the treasury's right cannot be nullified by an agent's statement.

Credibility of Dhimmi and Harbi and the Exception of Umm Walad
Rules of credibility for dhimmi and harbi, with the umm walad exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dhimmi is believed in financial claims, when a harbi is not, and explain the sole exception of umm walad.

Core Idea
A dhimmi is trusted like a Muslim in transactions, but not in jizya; a harbi is not trusted except for acknowledging his umm walad.

This lesson covers the rules of credibility for non-Muslims in financial matters before a Muslim authority. The Hanafi school makes a distinction between dhimmis (protected non-Muslims) and harbis (enemies), and further distinguishes between ordinary transactions and specific claims like jizya. There is also a unique exception: a harbi's acknowledgment of his slave-girl as umm walad is accepted.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for the dhimmi in transactions
A dhimmi is believed in transactions and their rulings, just like a Muslim. This is because they are in the 'abode of Islam' and participate in commerce.
This applies to claims of payment, debts, etc.
2
Identify the exception for jizya
A dhimmi is not believed if he claims he has paid his jizya. The reason is that jizya is a fixed obligation on him, and his claim alone would nullify the ruler's right to collect it.
The text says: 'except he is not believed concerning jizya if he says: I have paid it.'
3
Explain the rule for the harbi
A harbi (enemy non-Muslim) is not believed in such claims at all. This is because he is not under Islamic jurisdiction and is likely to be dishonest to avoid payment.
The general rule: 'the harbi is not believed.'
4
Analyze the exception for umm walad
Even a harbi is believed if he claims his slave-girl is his umm walad (mother of his child). This is because the acknowledgment relates to lineage and personal status, which is valid from any person in possession.
The text says: 'he is believed because his acknowledgment of lineage of one in his possession is valid, and being a harbi does not conflict with istilad.'
5
Connect to the 'Ashir context
These rules are given in the context of the 'Ashir's claims. The 'Ashir may bring a dhimmi or harbi before the authority. The credibility rules determine whether the 'Ashir's claim or the merchant's claim prevails.
The exception of umm walad is also tied to the 'Ashir: if the 'Ashir seizes a slave-girl and a harbi says she is his umm walad, the harbi is believed.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the credibility of the 'Ashir (tax collector) when he claims to have taken the 'ushr (tithe) from a merchant passing the frontier. The Hanafi position is that the 'Ashir is not believed in his claim, and the 'ushr is taken from the merchant a second time, except in the case of a claim regarding an umm walad (a slave-girl who has borne her master a child). The core reasoning is that the 'Ashir acts as an agent of the ruler, and his taking is an authoritative act; invalidating it would lead to loss of public funds, analogous to jizya or a debt paid to a minor's guardian where the guardian can take it again. Al-Shafi'i disagreed, arguing that the 'Ashir delivered the right to its owner. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the 'Ashir's claim is against the interest of the public treasury, so he is not trusted. For the dhimmi, he is believed in normal transactions but not in claiming jizya payment because the jizya is a fixed obligation on him and his claim would nullify the ruler's right. The exception of am walad is because the acknowledgment of lineage from a man to a child in his possession is valid even if he is a harbi, as it relates to personal status.

Key Terms
العاشر (al-'āshir): The tax collector at the frontier who collects 'ushr (tithe) on goods entering the Muslim territory.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child from her master, the child is free and she becomes free upon the master's death.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under protection in an Islamic state, paying jizya.

B) Because jizya is a fixed obligation, and his claim would nullify the ruler's right to collect it.
C) Because dhimmis are inherently dishonest about taxes.
D) Because the 'Ashir has better knowledge.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi is believed in transactions except jizya; a harbi is not believed except for the acknowledgment of umm walad, which concerns lineage.

Conditions for 'Ushr from Harbi: Nisab and Reciprocity
Sets conditions for taking 'ushr from a harbi including nisab and reciprocity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for taking 'ushr from a harbi and apply the principle of reciprocity to determine the amount taken.

Core Idea
The 'ushr from a harbi is conditioned on nisab and reciprocity—if they take nothing, we take nothing.

In the Hanafi school, the 'ushr (10% tax) on a harbi merchant entering Muslim territory is not an absolute rule. It is tied to two conditions: the harbi's goods must reach the nisab (minimum zakat threshold), and his state must impose a similar tax on Muslim merchants. This reflects the principle of justice and noble conduct. Let us unpack the ruling and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its two conditions
The text states that 'ushr is taken from a harbi 'on condition of nisab and their taking from us.' Recognize these as the two prerequisites for the obligation.
The conditions are separate: nisab applies to both Muslims and dhimmis; reciprocity is specific to harbi.
2
Understand the reciprocity principle
Reciprocity means we only take what the harbi's state takes from our merchants. If they take 5%, we take 5%; if they take nothing, we take nothing. This is based on the idea that we are 'more deserving of noble qualities' (أحق بالمكارم).
The default rate when unknown is a full 10%, based on 'Umar's instruction.
3
Apply the default rule from 'Umar
When the exact rate taken by the harbi's state is unknown, the 'Ashir takes a full tenth. This is from the saying of 'Umar: 'If you are unable (to know), then the tenth.'
This is a practical solution for uncertainty.
4
Recognize the rule on frequency and return to dar al-harb
The 'ushr is not taken twice from the same harbi within one year unless he leaves Muslim territory (returns to dar al-harb) and re-enters, which renews his aman (safe conduct).
The aman ends upon return to dar al-harb, so a new entry triggers a new assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخذ منا ربع العشر، ومن الذمي ضعفه ومن الحربي العشر بشرط نصاب وأخذهم منا، ولم يثن في حول بلا عود. بشرط نصاب وأخذهم منا: هذا الكلام من قبيل اللف والنشر المرتب، فقوله: "بشرط نصاب" متعلق بقوله: "وأخذ منا ومن الذمي"، وقوله: "وأخذهم منا" يتعلق بقوله: "ومن الحربي" أي نأخذ منه العشر بشرط أخذهم العشر منا، حتى لو مر حربي بخمسين درهماً أو بمائتي درهم لم يؤخذ منهم شيء، إلا أن يأخذوا منا من مثلها، وفي كتاب الزكاة: لا يؤخذ من القليل وإن أخذوا منا من مثله، وإن مر بنصاب ولم يعلم كم يأخذون منا يؤخذ منه العشر؛ لقول عمر رضي الله عنه: فإن أعياكم فالعشر، وإن علم أنهم يأخذون منا ربع عشر أو نصف عشر نأخذ بقدره، وإن لم يأخذوا منا لا نأخذ منهم؛ لأنا أحق بالمكارم، وإن كانوا يأخذون الكل لا نأخذ الكل، بل نبقى معه ما يوصله إلى المأمن. (مسكين وغيره) نصاب: أي بشرط بلوغ ماله نصاباً فلا يؤخذ من القليل وإن أخذوا منا من مثله. (ط، ع) منا: فلو لم يأخذوا منا لا نأخذ شيئاً منه، وإن أخذوا الكل لا نأخذ الكل بل نبقى معه ما يبلغه إلى مامنه. (ط) ولم يثن أي لم يأخذ العاشر العشر من الحربي ثانياً. (ط) بلا عود إلى دار الحرب، فلو عاد ثم خرج عشر ثانياً ] حتى لو مر حربي على عاشر فعشره ثم مرة أخرى لم يعشره حتى يحول الحول، وإن عشره فرجع إلى دار الحرب ثم خرج من يومه ذلك عشر ثانياً؛ لأنه بالرجوع ينتهي الأمان، وله الأمان ما دام في دارنا، وبالرجوع إلى دار الحرب يتجدد له الأمان بأمان جديد. (ع)
He takes from us a quarter of a tenth, from the dhimmi double that, and from the harbi a tenth, on condition of nisab and their taking from us, and he does not take it twice in a year without return. On condition of nisab and their taking from us: This is an example of laf wa nashr murattab (matched and ordered exposition). His statement 'on condition of nisab' relates to his statement 'He takes from us and from the dhimmi', and his statement 'and their taking from us' relates to his statement 'and from the harbi', meaning we take the tenth from him on condition that they take the tenth from us. So if a harbi passes with fifty dirhams or two hundred dirhams, nothing is taken from them, unless they take from us the like. In Kitab al-Zakat: nothing is taken from a small amount even if they take from us its like. If he passes with nisab and we do not know how much they take from us, the tenth is taken from him due to the saying of 'Umar may Allah be pleased with him: 'If you are unable, then the tenth.' If it is known that they take from us a quarter of a tenth or half a tenth, we take accordingly. If they do not take from us, we do not take from them, because we are more deserving of noble qualities. If they take all, we do not take all, rather we leave with him enough to reach safety. (Miskin etc.) Nisab: meaning on condition that his wealth reaches nisab, so nothing is taken from a small amount even if they take from us its like. (T, A) From us: if they do not take from us, we do not take anything from him, and if they take all, we do not take all, rather we leave with him enough to reach safety. (T) And he does not double: meaning the 'Ashir does not take the tenth from a harbi a second time. (T) Without return to dar al-harb: if he returns and then leaves, he is tithed a second time. So if a harbi passes an 'Ashir who tithes him, then again another time, he is not tithed until the year passes. If he is tithed and then returns to dar al-harb, then leaves on the same day, he is tithed a second time, because by returning the aman ends, and he has aman as long as he is in our territory, and by returning to dar al-harb a new aman is renewed. (A)
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and exposition of the conditions for taking 'ushr from a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) entering Muslim territory. The core idea is reciprocity: we take from the harbi only if he takes a similar amount from us. The text explains that the 'Ashir takes a quarter of a tenth (2.5%) from Muslims, double that (5%) from dhimmis, and a tenth (10%) from harbis, but only if the harbi's goods reach the nisab (minimum amount for zakat, typically 200 dirhams or 5 camels) and only if the harbi's state takes a similar tax from Muslim merchants. The principle is based on justice and reciprocity. If we don't know their rate, we default to a tenth based on a statement of Caliph 'Umar. If they take less, we take the same. If they take nothing, we take nothing (we are more noble). If they take all, we leave enough for the harbi to return safely. Additionally, the 'ushr is not taken twice from the same person in one year unless he leaves Muslim territory and returns, which renews his aman (safe conduct) and triggers a new assessment. The proof is from the practice of 'Umar and rational analogy.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim from enemy territory (dar al-harb) with whom there is no treaty or aman.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers zakat (200 dirhams or 5 camels for grazing livestock).
أمان (aman): Safe conduct or temporary protection granted to a harbi entering Muslim territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if a harbi's state takes no tax from Muslim merchants, what does the 'Ashir do when a harbi with nisab-level goods enters?
A) Take a full tenth (10%) because it is the default.
B) Take nothing because of reciprocity.
C) Take only a quarter of a tenth (2.5%) like a Muslim.
D) Take half a tenth (5%) because he is not a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The 'ushr from a harbi is determined by reciprocity and nisab; if his state takes nothing, we take nothing.

The Ruling on 'Ushr for Wine and Swine: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States the 'ushr ruling and maps the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'ushr for wine and swine, explain the intra-Hanafi differences, and identify the mufta bihi position based on the evidence from 'Umar's practice.

Core Idea
Wine has commercial value for dhimmis; swine has no allowable value, so 'ushr is taken on wine only.

This passage addresses a practical question for the 'Ashir (tithe collector): when a dhimmi or harbi enters Muslim territory with goods subject to 'ushr, how should wine and swine be treated? While both are impure in Islamic law, the Hanafi school recognizes that wine holds commercial value among non-Muslims, whereas swine does not. This leads to a nuanced ruling with internal disagreements among Hanafi imams and a contrasting view from al-Shafi'i. We will trace the evidence from 'Umar's directive and understand the practical consequence of each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
The core question is: does the 'Ashir take 'ushr on wine and/or swine when a dhimmi or harbi passes with them? The ruling statement says 'ushr on wine, not on swine.
This is a matter of zakah on trade goods from non-Muslims.
2
Map the positions
List the four positions: (1) Majority Hanafis (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad): 'ushr on wine only; (2) Zufar: 'ushr on both; (3) Abu Yusuf: 'ushr on both if together, only wine if separate; (4) al-Shafi'i: no 'ushr on either.
Note that Abu Yusuf's view is a partial agreement with Zufar.
3
Analyze the evidence
The Hanafi majority relies on 'Umar's instruction to sell the wine of dhimmis and take 'ushr from the price. This shows wine has recognized value. Swine has no such precedent.
The evidence is a practice of a companion (athar).
4
Determine the value assessment
Wine's value is determined by the word of upright dhimmis or converts, or by consulting the dhimmi community. Swine has no value in Islamic law.
This explains why 'ushr is taken on wine by value, not by volume.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a dhimmi passes with wine, the 'Ashir takes 'ushr on its value; if with swine, nothing is taken; if with both, the mufta bihi view is only wine is tithed.
The 'Ashir must be aware of the different rulings to act correctly.

Source Text

وعشر الخمر، لا الخنزير: [ أي لا يعشر الخنزير، ولو مع الخمر.] وعند الشافعي: لا يعشرهما جميعاً، وقال زفر: يعشرهما جميعاً، وقال أبو يوسف: إن مر بهما جميعا عشرا، وإن مر بهما على الانفراد عشر الخمر لا الخنزير، ولهما: أن عمر رضي الله عنه قال لعامله في خمور أهل الذمة: ولوهم ببيعها، وخذوا العشر من أثمانها، وإنما تعرف قيمة الخمر بقول فاسقين تابعين، أو ذميين أسلما، ويقال: يعرف ذلك بالرجوع إلى أهل الذمة. [رمز الحقائق: ١٢٦/١]
And the 'ushr of wine, not swine: [meaning swine is not tithed, even if together with wine.] And according to al-Shafi'i: he does not tithe both of them. Zufar said: he tithes both of them. Abu Yusuf said: if they pass together, he tithes both; if they pass separately, he tithes wine but not swine. And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): 'Umar may Allah be pleased with him said to his agent concerning the wines of ahl al-dhimma: 'Entrust them with selling it, and take the tenth from its prices.' And the value of wine is only known by the statement of two upright transgressors or of dhimmis who have embraced Islam. It is said: that is known by referring to the ahl al-dhimma. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/126]
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether 'ushr is taken on wine and swine (khinzir) when a dhimmi or harbi passes with them. The ruling states that 'ushr is taken on wine (by value) but not on swine. Al-Shafi'i exempts both, Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) imposes 'ushr on both, and Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if they pass together, both are tithed; if separately, only wine is tithed. The majority position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) follows the ruling: 'ushr on wine, not on swine. The evidence is from the practice of 'Umar who instructed that wine belonging to dhimmis be sold and the tithe taken from the price. This indicates that wine has commercial value in Islamic law for the purpose of 'ushr. Swine, however, has no value and is not subject to trade, so no 'ushr is taken. The dispute also involves how to determine the value of wine: by the word of two upright dhimmis who have converted, or by reference to the dhimmi community.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine (intoxicating drink), which is impure for Muslims but can be owned and traded by non-Muslims in their dealings.
خنزير (khinzir): Swine (pig), which is impure and has no permissible market value for Muslims or dhimmis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what does the 'Ashir do when a dhimmi passes with wine only?
A) Takes nothing because wine is impure.
B) Takes the 'ushr on the value of the wine.
C) Takes the 'ushr on the wine itself (by volume).
D) Destroys the wine and fines the dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes wine from swine for 'ushr based on commercial value: wine is tithed by value, swine is not, following the precedent of 'Umar.

Exemptions from 'Ushr: Wealth in Home, Bida'a, Mudaraba, and Ma'dhun's Earnings
Lists exemptions from frontier 'ushr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories exempt from 'ushr and explain the legal reasons (ownership and protection) that justify their exemption.

Core Idea
The 'Ashir only taxes wealth brought under his protection and owned by the passer.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lists four types of wealth that are not subject to 'ushr when crossing a frontier checkpoint. Understanding these exemptions reveals the Hanafi principle that frontier tax applies only to wealth that is both owned by the person crossing and physically presented for inspection. Hidden wealth, partnerships where the carrier is not the owner, and slave earnings held by a master all fall outside this scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four exempt categories
From the text, list the four items that are not tithed: 1) wealth kept in home (not brought), 2) bida'a (worker is volunteer), 3) mudaraba capital (mudarib is not owner), 4) earnings of ma'dhun slave (ownership belongs to master).
2
Understand the legal rationale for each
The text explains: hidden wealth (in home) is not under the 'Ashir's protection; bida'a worker is a volunteer, not owner; mudarib is not owner of capital, only profits; ma'dhun's earnings belong to master.
Note the evolution of Abu Hanifa's view on mudaraba capital.
3
Extract the operative maxim
The overarching rule: 'Ushr is only taken from wealth that is brought under the 'Ashir's physical protection and is owned by the person passing. Exemptions arise when ownership is absent or wealth is not presented.
This maxim is derived from the specific rulings.

(ع)] "وما في بيته" إلى "كسب" "المأذون في محل النصب" عطف على قوله لا الخنزير والبضاعة وما بعدها عطف على "ما في بيته"، أي لو مر على العاشر ذمي أو مسلم بأقل من مائتي درهم وأخبره أن له في منزله ما يبلغ نصاباً، وقد حال عليه الحول لم يأخذ العاشر منه شيئاً؛ لأن حق الأخذ في الأموال الباطنة مقيد بالمرور بها؛ ولأن ما في بيته لم يدخل تحت حمايته. ولا يعشر البضاعة وهي ما يكون الربح فيها للمالك وحده بأن يكون العامل متبرعاً بعمله. ولا يعشر أيضاً لو مرّ بمال المضاربة أي رأس مالها، وكان أبو حنيفة يقول أولا بعشره؛ لقوة حق المضاربة حتى لا يملك رب المال نهيه عن التصرف فيه بعد ما صار عروضاً، فينزل بمنزلة المالك، ثم رجع وقال: لا يعشره، وهو قولهما؛ لأن المضارب ليس بمالك، ولا نائب في رأس المال إلا إذا ربح وبلغ نصيبه نصاباً، فإنه يعشر نصيبه. وكذا لو مر عبد مأذون له لا يعشره ولو لم يكن عليه دين؛ لأن الملك فيما في يده للمولى، ومناط المضارب كونه ليس ولا نائباً عنه، وهذا موجود في المأذون ومجرد دخوله تحت الحماية لا يوجب الأخذ إلا مع شروطه. (مستخلص، فتح) والبضاعة: أي ولا يعشر أيضاً للبضاعة وهي المال الذي يدفعه الشخص إلى آخر ليكتسب منه وليس عليه شيء؛ لأنه ليس بمالك ولا نائب عنه في أداء الزكاة. (ع) مال المضاربة: وهو الذي يدفعه شخص إلى آخر ليتجر فيه ويكون الربح بينهما. وكسب المأذون: أي لا يعشر أيضاً كسب العبد المأذون له في التجارة إذا مر به على العاشر. (ع)
And what is in his home, and the bida'a, and the capital of mudaraba, and the earnings of the ma'dhun. Not swine: [meaning what is in his home of wealth is not tithed even if it is nisab, because it has not come under protection. (A)] 'And what is in his home' to 'earnings' 'the ma'dhun' is in the accusative case as a conjunct to 'not swine', and 'bida'a' and what follows are conjuncts to 'what is in his home'. That is, if a dhimmi or Muslim passes the 'Ashir with less than two hundred dirhams and informs him that he has in his home wealth that reaches nisab and a year has passed, the 'Ashir does not take anything, because the right to take on hidden wealth is conditional upon passing with it, and because what is in his home has not come under his protection. And the bida'a is not tithed, which is where the profit is solely for the owner, the worker being a volunteer. Also the capital of mudaraba is not tithed if he passes with it. Abu Hanifa initially said it is tithed due to the strength of the mudarib's right, to the extent that the owner cannot forbid him from dealing after it becomes goods, so he is like an owner. Then he retracted and said it is not tithed, which is the position of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the mudarib is not an owner and is not a deputy in the capital unless he makes a profit and his share reaches nisab, then his share is tithed. Similarly, if a permitted slave (ma'dhun) passes, he is not tithed even if he has no debt, because the ownership of what is in his hand belongs to the master, and the basis of the mudarib is that he is not an owner nor a deputy, and this is also true for the ma'dhun. Mere entry under protection does not necessitate taking except with its conditions. (Mukhtasar, Fath) Bida'a: meaning it is also not tithed, which is wealth given by one person to another to trade with, with no liability on the worker, because he is not an owner nor a deputy in paying zakat. (A) Mudaraba capital: wealth given by one to another to trade with, profit shared between them. Earnings of the ma'dhun: meaning the earnings of a slave permitted to trade are also not tithed when he passes the 'Ashir. (A)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing exemptions from 'ushr at the frontier. The 'Ashir does not take 'ushr on: (1) wealth that remains in the merchant's home (hidden wealth not brought to the checkpoint), even if it reaches nisab, because it is not under the protection of the 'Ashir; (2) bida'a (a type of partnership where the worker volunteers and takes no profit share, so he is not the owner); (3) the capital of mudaraba (an investment partnership) because the mudarib is not the owner; only if the mudarib's profit share reaches nisab is his share tithed; (4) earnings of a ma'dhun slave, because ownership remains with the master. Abu Hanifa initially considered mudaraba capital subject to 'ushr but later retracted to align with the Sahibayn, showing the evolution of Hanafi rulings. The core principle is that the 'Ashir only takes from wealth that is brought under his physical protection and is owned by the person passing. Hidden wealth not brought is not taxed, and ownership is a key factor.

Key Terms
بضاعة (bida'a): A type of trade where one person gives capital to another to trade, with all profit to the owner; the worker is a volunteer.
مضاربة (mudaraba): A partnership where one party provides capital, the other provides labor, and profit is shared according to agreement.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not owned by the mudarib; he is a trustee.
B) Because it is not nisab.
C) Because the mudarib has already paid zakat.
D) Because it is considered hidden wealth.

Key Takeaway
The 'Ashir's right to tax depends on ownership and physical presentation; wealth not meeting these conditions is exempt from 'ushr.

Double Tithe from Merchants Passing Khawarij
Ruling with reasoning on double 'ushr due to merchant's negligence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where payment to outlaws exempts from further zakat based on the factor of negligence (taqsir) and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
A merchant who voluntarily pays 'ushr to Khawarij must pay again to the just ruler because his negligence forfeits the exemption.

This passage addresses an apparent contradiction within Hanafi fiqh: why does a merchant who pays 'ushr to Khawarij have to pay again to the just ruler, while a livestock owner whose animals are forcibly tithed by Khawarij does not? The key lies in the concept of taqsir (negligence). The merchant voluntarily entered Khawarij territory and thus is considered a wrongdoer, so the right of the poor is not nullified. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying zakat rules in situations of political instability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Contrast the merchant case (voluntary passage through Khawarij) with the livestock case (Khawarij overpower the town and take zakat by force).
The ruling appears contradictory until the basis of distinction is clarified.
2
Locate the cause of distinction (taqsir)
Recognize that negligence (taqsir) is attributed to the person who caused the situation. In the merchant case, the merchant chose to pass the Khawarij; in the livestock case, the owners had no choice.
Taqsir determines whether the obligation to the poor is discharged or not.
3
Apply the principle to the merchant case
Since the merchant is negligent (he passed them voluntarily), his payment to Khawarij does not fulfill the right of the poor. He must pay 'ushr again to the just ruler.
The right of the poor is like a debt that cannot be invalidated by the payer's own fault.
4
Apply the principle to the livestock case
Since the livestock owners did not voluntarily go to the Khawarij (they were forced), there is no negligence on them. The negligence is on the imam for failing to protect. Thus, they do not pay again.
This preserves the principle that forced payment does not discharge the obligation without negligence.
5
Synthesize the general rule
Formulate the rule: If a person voluntarily submits to an illegitimate authority and pays zakat/tax, the obligation to the legitimate authority remains. If payment is forced without choice, the obligation is discharged (assuming no collusion).
This rule protects the poor from being deprived due to the payer's misconduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثني إن عشر الخوارج: أي إن مر تاجر بعاشر الخوارج وعشروا منه ثم مر على عاشر أهل العدل عشر ثانياً، لا يقال: هذا مناقض لما ذكره قبله في باب صدقة السوائم، وهو إذا أخذ العشر بغاة لا يؤخذ أخرى؛ لأن التقصير ههنا منه حيث مر عليهم فكان جانياً فلا يبطل به حق الفقير، بخلاف ما إذا غلب الخوارج على بلد وأخذوا زكاة سوائمهم فإنه لا شيء عليهم؛ لأنه لا تقصير منهم وإنما التقصير من الإمام. (مسكين)
And double if the Khawarij take the tenth: meaning if a merchant passes the 'Ashir of the Khawarij and they tithe him, then he passes the 'Ashir of the just people, he is tithed a second time. It should not be said: this contradicts what he mentioned earlier in the chapter of zakat on grazing livestock, where if outlaws take the tenth, it is not taken again, because here the negligence is from him; he passed them and thus was a wrongdoer, so the right of the poor is not nullified. Unlike if the Khawarij overcome a town and take zakat on their livestock, then nothing is owed on them because there is no negligence from them, rather the negligence is from the imam. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning on the situation of a merchant who passes through a territory controlled by the Khawarij (rebels/outlaws) and pays 'ushr to them, and then passes a legitimate 'Ashir of the just ruler. The ruling is that the legitimate 'Ashir takes 'ushr again from the merchant. This may seem to contradict the earlier rule in the section on zakat on grazing livestock (sadaqat al-sawa'im), where if outlaws take the zakat, it is not taken again. The distinction is that in the case of the livestock, the owner did not willingly go to the outlaws; rather, the outlaws came and took by force (they overcame the town). There, the negligence is from the imam for failing to protect. In the case of the merchant passing through the Khawarij voluntarily, he is negligent (he is a 'jani' - wrongdoer by choice), so the right of the poor (the zakat/ushr) is not forfeited; he must pay again. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of taqsir (negligence) affecting liability.

Key Terms
خوارج (Khawarij): Outlaws or rebels who take taxes by force and are not legitimate rulers.
تقصير (taqsir): Negligence or fault of the person causing the situation; here the merchant is at fault for choosing to pass the Khawarij.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a merchant voluntarily passes through Khawarij territory and pays them 'ushr, then passes a just 'Ashir, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He does not pay again because he already paid 'ushr.
B) He pays 'ushr again because he was negligent by choosing to pass them.
C) He pays only half because the Khawarij took some.
D) He is excused because the Khawarij forced him.

Key Takeaway
The key is the distinction based on the presence or absence of the payer's negligence: voluntary payment to outlaws does not discharge the zakat obligation.

Items Exempt from 'Ushr: Pig, Household Wealth, and Partnership Capital
Listing exemptions from 'ushr with proofs and intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories exempt from 'ushr and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pig and wine.

Core Idea
The 'ushr collector takes only from wealth physically passed under his protection.

This lesson examines specific exemptions from the 'ushr tax as detailed in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The passage lists five categories of wealth that are not tithed when carried past the collector, even if they meet nisab. Understanding these exemptions is crucial for applying the 'ushr system correctly, as it protects merchants from double taxation and clarifies the scope of the collector's authority.

Guided Path
1
List the five exempt categories
Identify the five items that the passage explicitly states the tithe collector does not take: pig, wealth in the house, bida'a (commission trade), mudaraba capital, and earnings of a ma'dhun (permitted slave).
2
Analyze the pig and wine dispute
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding pig and wine. Note that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold neither is tithed, Zufar says both are, and Abu Yusuf distinguishes between separate and simultaneous passage.
The proof for the Hanafi position is the practice of Caliph 'Umar.
3
Understand the rationale for household wealth
Wealth remaining in the owner's home is not tithed because it has not come under the collector's protection. The right to collect 'ushr applies only to goods physically carried past the collector.
4
Apply the double tithe rule
If a merchant voluntarily passes a Kharijite collector and pays 'ushr, the payment does not exempt him from paying again to a just collector, due to his negligence. This contrasts with forced seizure by rebels.
The key difference is the owner's negligence.

Book of Zakat. Chapter of the Tithe Collector. Not the pig, and what is in his house, and the commission trade, and the capital of mudaraba, and the earnings of the permitted slave, and the double tithe of the Kharijites. That is, the tithe is taken a second time. Not the pig: [i.e., the pig is not tithed, even if with wine.] According to al-Shafi'i: he does not tithe both. Zufar said: he tithes both. Abu Yusuf said: if they pass together, he tithes both; if they pass separately, he tithes the wine, not the pig. And their proof: that 'Umar said to his agent regarding the wines of the people of dhimmah: 'Give them permission to sell them, and take the tithe from their prices.' And the value of wine is known by the statement of two apostates, or two dhimmis who have become Muslim. And it is said: that is known by referring to the people of dhimmah. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/126] And what is in his house: [i.e., also do not tithe what is in the house of the passer-by of wealth, even if it is nisab, because it has not come under protection. (A)] 'And what is in his house' to 'earnings' 'the permitted slave is in the accusative case, conjoined to 'not the pig' and 'the commission trade' and what follows is conjoined to 'what is in his house'. That is, if a dhimmi or Muslim passes by the tithe collector with less than two hundred dirhams and informs him that he has in his house what reaches nisab and a year has passed, the tithe collector does not take anything from him; because the right of taking in hidden wealth is conditional upon passing with it; and because what is in his house has not come under his protection. And the commission trade is not tithed: it is wealth where the profit is for the owner alone, as the worker volunteers his work. And also not tithed if one passes with mudaraba capital, i.e., its principal. Abu Hanifa initially said it is tithed, due to the strength of the mudaraba right, such that the owner of the capital cannot forbid him from disposing of it after it becomes goods, so he is like the owner. Then he retracted and said: it is not tithed, and this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; because the mudarib is not an owner, nor a deputy regarding the principal, unless he makes a profit and his share reaches nisab, then he tithes his share. Likewise, if a permitted slave passes, he is not tithed, even if he has no debt; because ownership of what is in his hand is for the master, and the basis regarding the mudarib is that he is neither an owner nor a deputy for him, and this exists in the permitted slave. And mere entering under protection does not necessitate taking except with its conditions. (Mukhtalas, Fath) And the commission trade: i.e., also do not tithe the commission trade, which is wealth that a person gives to another to earn from, and he is not liable for anything; because he is not an owner nor a deputy for him in paying zakat. (A) Mudaraba capital: which a person gives to another to trade with, and the profit is between them. And the earnings of the permitted slave: i.e., also do not tithe the earnings of a slave permitted to trade if he passes by the tithe collector. (A) And 'double' etc.: i.e., if a merchant passes by a Kharijite tithe collector and they tithe him, then he passes by a tithe collector of the people of justice, he is tithed a second time. It is not said: this contradicts what he mentioned earlier in the chapter of zakat on grazing animals, which is if the rebels take the tithe, it is not taken again. Because the shortcoming here is from him where he passed by them, so he was negligent, so the right of the poor is not nullified. Unlike if the Kharijites dominate a land and take zakat on their grazing animals, then nothing is due on them, because there is no shortcoming from them, but the shortcoming is from the imam. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details which items are exempt from the 'ushr (tithe) when carried past the tithe collector. The chapter opens by listing five exempt categories: pig, household wealth in the owner's house, commission trade goods (bida'a), mudaraba capital, and earnings of a slave permitted to trade. Additionally, it addresses the case of double tithe when a merchant passes first by a Kharijite collector and then by a just collector.

The first ruling clarifies that pig is not subject to 'ushr, even if accompanied by wine. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar holds both are tithed, Abu Yusuf says both are tithed if together but only wine if separate, and the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that neither is tithed. The proof is the practice of Caliph 'Umar who allowed dhimmis to sell their wine and took tithe from the price, not the wine itself. The value of wine is determined by the testimony of two apostates or Muslim dhimmis, or by referring to dhimmis.

Second, wealth inside a person's house (even if nisab) is not tithed when the person passes the collector without the wealth, because the right to take applies only to wealth brought under the collector's protection (i.e., carried past).

Third, bida'a (commission trade) where the worker is a volunteer not liable for loss is not tithed because the worker is neither owner nor deputy for zakat.

Fourth, mudaraba capital: Abu Hanifa initially ruled it is tithed due to the mudarib's strong right, but later retracted to agree with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that it is not tithed, as the mudarib is not the owner. However, if the mudarib's profit share reaches nisab, he tithes his profit.

Fifth, earnings of a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun) are not tithed because ownership remains with the master, and the slave is not a deputy for zakat.

Finally, the double tithe ruling: if a merchant voluntarily passes through Kharijite territory and pays 'ushr, he must pay again when passing a just collector, because his negligence does not cancel the poor's right. This differs from the case where rebels forcibly take zakat on grazing animals, in which no second payment is required because the owner was not negligent.

Key Terms
العاشر (al-'āshir): The official tithe collector appointed by the Islamic state to collect 'ushr from Muslim and dhimmi merchants
الخنزير (al-khinzīr): Pig meat, which is not subject to 'ushr because it is not a valuable (muttaqawwim) property for Muslims
المضاربة (al-muḍārabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profit shared as agreed
المأذون (al-ma’dhūn): A slave whom the master permits to engage in trade; his earnings belong to the master, so he is not liable for zakat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if a dhimmi passes the tithe collector carrying only a pig, what is taken?
A) Nothing
B) The full 'ushr
C) Half 'ushr
D) Double 'ushr

Key Takeaway
The 'ushr collector's authority is limited to goods physically carried past him, and certain items like pig, household wealth, and partnership capital are exempt regardless of value.

Khums on Minerals and Treasure: Hanafi Rulings on Rikaz
Defining rikaz and ruling on khums for minerals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khums for minerals, identify the conditions that trigger khums, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i/Maliki view.

Core Idea
Khums is wajib on meltable minerals found in kharaj or 'ushr land, based on the hadith 'In rikaz is a fifth.'

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on Rikaz (treasure trove). It defines rikaz as anything under the ground, whether a natural mine (ma'din) or buried treasure (kanz). The core ruling is that khums (one-fifth) is wajib on meltable minerals like gold, silver, and iron, but only if they are found in land subject to kharaj or 'ushr. The passage also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute on minerals found in one's own house or land and a cross-madhhab dispute with Malik and al-Shafi'i, who consider rikaz subject to zakat instead of khums.

Guided Path
1
Define rikaz and its types
Recognize that rikaz encompasses both natural mines (ma'din) and buried treasure (kanz). Understand that the Hanafi definition includes anything under the ground, whether created by Allah or buried by people.
The term rikaz is derived from 'rakz' meaning establishment; it is not limited to buried treasure.
2
Identify the subject of khums
Khums is wajib on meltable metals (e.g., gold, silver, iron, lead) found in kharaj or 'ushr land. Non-meltable solids (e.g., antimony, stones) and liquids (e.g., tar, oil) are excluded.
This classification is specific to the Hanafi school.
3
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith 'Wa fi al-rikaz al-khumus' (In rikaz is a fifth). The Hanafi school applies this to mines by analogy to spoils of war (ghanimah), as mines were in the hands of unbelievers and taken by conquest.
The hadith is considered explicit (nass) by the Hanafis.
4
Note the land condition and exemption
Khums is only due on minerals found in kharaj or 'ushr land, not in Dar al-Harb. Additionally, on one narration from Abu Hanifa, no khums is due on minerals found in one's own house or land; another narration says khums is required due to land charges.
The exemption for the house (and shop) is stated explicitly: 'لا في داره'.
5
Compare with other schools
Malik and al-Shafi'i hold that rikaz is not subject to khums; rather, if it is gold/silver and reaches nisab, zakat is due without the requirement of a year (hawl). The Hanafi response is the explicit hadith and the analogy to ghanimah.
The practical difference: Hanafi gives 1/5 to treasury; Shafi'i/Maliki gives 2.5% as zakat after nisab.

Source Text

باب الركاز
خمس معدن نقد ونحو حديد في أرض خراج أو عشر، لا في داره وأرضه .
أخذت
منهم
وهو الذهب والفضة
معدنا
باب الركاز : [ أي في بيان أحكامه هو اسم لما يكون تحت الأرض خلقة أو يدفنه العباد، فالأول يسمى والثاني كنزا. (ع)] حق هذا الباب أن يذكر في السير؛ لأن المأخوذ منه ليس زكاة وإنما يصرف مصرف الغنيمة، وإنما الحقوه بالزكاة؛ لكونه من الوظائف المالية فأشبه الزكاة، وقدمه على العشر؛ لأن العشر مؤونة فيها معنى القربة، والركاز قربة محضة، وهو بالكسر من الركز بمعنى الإثبات أعم من أن يكون راكزه الخالق أو المخلوق، فكان حقيقة في المعدن والكنز مشتركا معنوياً وليس خاصاً بالدفين. (فتح) خمس: بصيغة المجهول مخففا أي أخذ الخمس من معدن نقد، تقول: خمست القوم أخمسهم من باب نصر إذا خمس أموالهم، وشيء مخمّس بالتشديد له خمسة أركان كما في الصحاح، ومنه يعلم أن التشديد غير سديد؛ إذ لا معنى لكونه يجعل خمسة أخماس فقط، ووجوب الخمس في المعدن؛ لقوله عليه: "وفي الركاز الخمس"، وأطلق على المعدن؛ لأنه كان في أيدي الكفرة وحوته أيدينا غلبة، فكان غنيمة، وفي الغنيمة الخمس. (فتح) ونحو حديد: [ أي وخمس أيضاً نحو حديد، وأراد به كل جامد ينطبع بالنار كرصاص وصفر. (فتح)] احترز به عن المائعات كالقار والنفط والملح، اعلم أن ما يوجد تحت الأرض نوعان معدن وكنز، ولا تفصيل في الكنز، بل يجب فيه الخمس، سواء كان من جنس الأرض أم لم يكن بعد أن كان مالاً متقوماً؛ لأنه دفين الكفار، والمعدن ثلاثة أنواع: نوع يذوب بالنار وينطبع كالذهب والفضة وغيرهما، ونوع لا يذوب ولا ينطبع كالكحل وسائر الأحجار، ونوع يكون مائعاً كالقير والنفط، والوجوب يختص بالنوع الأول دون الآخرين. وقوله: "في أرض خراج وعشر" أي لو وجد شيء منها في أرض الخراج أو العشر ففيه الخمس، واحترز به عن الموجود في دار الحرب، فإن أرضها ليست أرض خراج ولا عشر وقال مالك والشافعي: لا يخمس الركاز؛ لأنه مباح سبقت يده إليه كالحطب ونحوه، إلا أنه إذا كان المستخرج ذهباً أو فضة وبلغ نصاباً تجب فيه الزكاة، ولا يشترط فيه الحول؛ لأنه للتنمية فأشبه الزرع، ولنا ما روينا: "وفي الركاز الخمس"، فالخمس لبيت المال، وأربعة أخماسه للواجد، ولو وجده في أرض مملوكة لغيره، فأربعة أخماس لمالك الرقبة وخمسه لبيت المال سواء كان الواجد حراً أو عبداً، مسلماً أو ذمياً بالغاً أو صبياً، ذكراً أو أنثى. (مسكين، فتح) أرض خراج أي إذا وجد في أرض خراجية أو عشرية، وباقيه للواجد. (ط) لا في داره: أي لا يؤخذ الخمس من معدن نقد ونحو حديد يوجد في داره، والحانوت كالدار؛ لأن المعدن من توابع الأرض بدليل دخوله في البيع بغير نص خلافاً لهما، وقوله: "لا" في أرضه عن أبي حنيفة روايتان في رواية "الأصل": لا يجب كما في داره؛ لأن من أجزاء الأرض، وليس في سائر الأجزاء منهما خمس، فكذا في هذا الجزء، وفي رواية "جامع الصغير": يجب؛ لأن الأرض لم تملك خالية عن المؤون بدليل وجوب العشر أو الخراج فيها بخلاف الدار. (فتح، مسكين)
Chapter of the Treasure Trove. A fifth is taken: from a mine of gold and silver and the like of iron, in kharaj or 'ushr land, not in his house or his land. And it is gold and silver. Mine. Chapter of the Treasure Trove: [i.e., regarding its rulings. It is a name for what is under the earth by creation or buried by people. The first is called mine, the second treasure. (A)] The proper place for this chapter is in the Book of Siyar (military expeditions), because what is taken from it is not zakat, but is spent in the same way as spoils (ghanimah). They attached it to zakat because it is among financial obligations, so it resembles zakat. He placed it before 'ushr because 'ushr involves a burden with a meaning of piety, while rikaz is pure piety. It is with a kasra (rikaz) from 'rakz' meaning establishment, general whether its establisher is the Creator or the created. So it is literal for mine and treasure, a homonym, not specific to buried treasure. (Fath) 'A fifth': in the passive voice, lightly pronounced, i.e., taking a fifth from a mine of gold and silver. You say: khamastu al-qawma akhmisuhum from the pattern nasara if you take a fifth of their wealth. A thing 'mukhamas' with shaddah has five corners as in al-Sihah. From this it is known that the shaddah is not correct, because there is no meaning that it makes only five-fifths. And the obligation of khums on mines is due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'And in rikaz is a fifth.' It was applied to mines because they were in the hands of the unbelievers and we took possession of them by conquest, so it was spoils, and in spoils is a fifth. (Fath) 'And the like of iron': [i.e., also a fifth on the like of iron. He meant by it every solid that can be melted by fire, like lead and brass. (Fath)] This excludes liquids like tar, oil, and salt. Know that what is found under the earth is of two types: mine and treasure (kanz). There is no detail in treasure; khums is obligatory on it, whether it is of the same material as the earth or not, provided it is valuable property (mal muttaqawwim), because it is buried property of the unbelievers. Mines are of three types: a type that melts by fire and can be shaped, like gold, silver, and others; a type that does not melt and cannot be shaped, like kohl (antimony) and other stones; and a type that is liquid, like tar and oil. The obligation is specific to the first type, not the other two. His statement: 'In kharaj or 'ushr land' means if something from these (metals) is found in kharaj or 'ushr land, then khums is due. This excludes what is found in Dar al-Harb. Malik and al-Shafi'i said: rikaz is not subject to khums, because it is mubah (ownerless) that one has taken possession of, like firewood and the like, except that if what is extracted is gold or silver and reaches nisab, zakat is due on it, and no year (hawl) is required because it is for growth, resembling crops. Our proof is what we narrated: 'And in rikaz is a fifth.' So the fifth is for the treasury (bayt al-mal), and four-fifths are for the finder. If he finds it in land owned by another, then four-fifths are for the owner of the land, and one-fifth for the treasury, whether the finder is free or slave, Muslim or dhimmi, adult or child, male or female. (Miskin, Fath) 'Kharaj land' means if found in kharaji or 'ushri land, and the remainder is for the finder. (T) 'Not in his house': i.e., khums is not taken from a mine of gold and silver and the like of iron found in his house. A shop is like a house, because the mine is a dependency of the land, as evidenced by its inclusion in a sale without explicit mention, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Regarding his statement 'not' in his land, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: in the narration of al-Asl, it is not obligatory as in his house, because it is part of the earth's components, and in other components there is no khums, so likewise in this part. In the narration of Jami' al-Saghir, it is obligatory, because the land is not owned free of charges, as evidenced by the obligation of 'ushr or kharaj on it, unlike the house. (Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Rikaz (treasure trove) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It defines rikaz as anything under the ground, either naturally occurring (mine) or buried (treasure). The chapter is placed under zakat but the ruling is closer to spoils of war; it is included here because it is a financial obligation.

The core ruling: khums (one-fifth) is obligatory on gold and silver mines and similar metals that can be melted and shaped (like iron, lead, copper), provided they are found in kharaj (taxed land) or 'ushr (tithed) land—i.e., land under Muslim jurisdiction, not in Dar al-Harb. The proof is the hadith: 'In rikaz is a fifth.' The passage explains that mines are treated as spoils because they were in the hands of unbelievers and taken by conquest.

Not subject to khums: liquids like tar, oil, salt; non-meltable solids like antimony and stones; any mineral found in one's own house or personal land (according to one narration from Abu Hanifa). There is an inter-madhhab dispute: Malik and al-Shafi'i argue that rikaz (including mines) is not subject to khums but rather zakat if it is gold/silver and reaches nisab, without needing a year. The Hanafi response is the explicit hadith.

Intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa has two narrations on minerals found in one's own land—one says no khums (as in the house), the other says yes because land carries charges ('ushr/kharaj) unlike a house. The relied-upon position seems to be the one exempting the house and land, but the text presents both.

Details: For treasure (kanz), khums is always due if it is valuable property of unbelievers, regardless of material. For mines, only meltable metals are subject. The fifth goes to the treasury, four-fifths to the finder or to the land owner if found on another's property.

Key Terms
الركاز (al-rikāz): Anything under the ground, whether created by Allah (mine) or buried by people (treasure)
المعدن (al-ma‘din): A natural mineral deposit; divided into three types: meltable and shapable (gold, silver, iron), non-meltable (antimony, stones), and liquid (tar, oil). Khums applies only to the first type.
الخمس (al-khumus): One-fifth; a financial obligation on rikaz, distinct from zakat, spent in the same categories as spoils of war (ghanimah)
أرض الخراج (arḍ al-kharāj): Land conquered by Muslims from unbelievers and left in their hands subject to an annual tax (kharaj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a gold mine found in one's own house?
A) Khums is obligatory
B) No khums
C) Zakat is obligatory
D) It is ownerless (mubah)

Key Takeaway
Khums is wajib on meltable minerals found in kharaj or 'ushr land, based on the hadith 'In rikaz is a fifth,' and this differs from the Shafi'i/Maliki view that only zakat applies.

Khums from Mines: Wajib on Malleable Minerals in Muslim Lands
States the Hanafi rule for khums on minerals and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khums from mines, identify the conditions (land type, mineral type), and explain the intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab disputes.

Core Idea
Khums from mines is wajib, not zakat, based on 'In rikaz is one-fifth', and applies only to malleable minerals in Muslim lands.

Mining and finding buried treasure raise important financial obligations. In the Hanafi school, these fall under khums (one-fifth) based on the Prophet's hadith. However, not every find is subject to khums—conditions regarding the land and the type of substance matter. This lesson unpacks the core ruling, its proof, and the scholarly differences within and outside the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is extracting minerals (ma'din) or finding treasure (kanz). The ruling is that khums (one-fifth) is wajib on the extracted rikaz under specific conditions.
Khums is a separate obligation from zakat; it is akin to spoils of war.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'وفي الركاز الخمس' (In rikaz is one-fifth). The passage also draws analogy to ghanimah because mines were previously owned by disbelievers and taken by force.
The hadith is ahad, so the obligation is wajib, not fard, in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The nominal sentence 'وفي الركاز الخمس' indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul—there is no qarina to the contrary. The analogy to ghanimah reinforces the ruling.
Other schools interpret the same text differently; some do not consider it a command for khums.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (intra-Hanafi)
There is disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding mines found in one's own house or land. Abu Hanifa has two narrations: in al-Asl no khums, in Jami' al-Saghir khums wajib. The Sahibayn hold khums wajib even in one's house.
The matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) prefers the narration of no khums in one's house.
5
Note the ikhtilaf (inter-madhhab)
Malik and al-Shafi'i do not require khums on rikaz; they consider it mubah property and only require zakat on gold/silver if nisab is reached, without haul.
The practical difference: Hanafis take khums from all rikaz meeting conditions; Malikis/Shafi'is only apply zakat on precious metals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الركاز : [ أي في بيان أحكامه هو اسم لما يكون تحت الأرض خلقة أو يدفنه العباد، فالأول يسمى والثاني كنزا. (ع)] حق هذا الباب أن يذكر في السير؛ لأن المأخوذ منه ليس زكاة وإنما يصرف مصرف الغنيمة، وإنما الحقوه بالزكاة؛ لكونه من الوظائف المالية فأشبه الزكاة، وقدمه على العشر؛ لأن العشر مؤونة فيها معنى القربة، والركاز قربة محضة، وهو بالكسر من الركز بمعنى الإثبات أعم من أن يكون راكزه الخالق أو المخلوق، فكان حقيقة في المعدن والكنز مشتركا معنوياً وليس خاصاً بالدفين. (فتح) خمس: بصيغة المجهول مخففا أي أخذ الخمس من معدن نقد، تقول: خمست القوم أخمسهم من باب نصر إذا خمس أموالهم، وشيء مخمّس بالتشديد له خمسة أركان كما في الصحاح، ومنه يعلم أن التشديد غير سديد؛ إذ لا معنى لكونه يجعل خمسة أخماس فقط، ووجوب الخمس في المعدن؛ لقوله عليه: "وفي الركاز الخمس"، وأطلق على المعدن؛ لأنه كان في أيدي الكفرة وحوته أيدينا غلبة، فكان غنيمة، وفي الغنيمة الخمس. (فتح) ونحو حديد: [ أي وخمس أيضاً نحو حديد، وأراد به كل جامد ينطبع بالنار كرصاص وصفر. (فتح)] احترز به عن المائعات كالقار والنفط والملح، اعلم أن ما يوجد تحت الأرض نوعان معدن وكنز، ولا تفصيل في الكنز، بل يجب فيه الخمس، سواء كان من جنس الأرض أم لم يكن بعد أن كان مالاً متقوماً؛ لأنه دفين الكفار، والمعدن ثلاثة أنواع: نوع يذوب بالنار وينطبع كالذهب والفضة وغيرهما، ونوع لا يذوب ولا ينطبع كالكحل وسائر الأحجار، ونوع يكون مائعاً كالقير والنفط، والوجوب يختص بالنوع الأول دون الآخرين. وقوله: "في أرض خراج وعشر" أي لو وجد شيء منها في أرض الخراج أو العشر ففيه الخمس، واحترز به عن الموجود في دار الحرب، فإن أرضها ليست أرض خراج ولا عشر وقال مالك والشافعي: لا يخمس الركاز؛ لأنه مباح سبقت يده إليه كالحطب ونحوه، إلا أنه إذا كان المستخرج ذهباً أو فضة وبلغ نصاباً تجب فيه الزكاة، ولا يشترط فيه الحول؛ لأنه للتنمية فأشبه الزرع، ولنا ما روينا: "وفي الركاز الخمس"، فالخمس لبيت المال، وأربعة أخماسه للواجد، ولو وجده في أرض مملوكة لغيره، فأربعة أخماس لمالك الرقبة وخمسه لبيت المال سواء كان الواجد حراً أو عبداً، مسلماً أو ذمياً بالغاً أو صبياً، ذكراً أو أنثى. (مسكين، فتح) أرض خراج أي إذا وجد في أرض خراجية أو عشرية، وباقيه للواجد. (ط) لا في داره: أي لا يؤخذ الخمس من معدن نقد ونحو حديد يوجد في داره، والحانوت كالدار؛ لأن المعدن من توابع الأرض بدليل دخوله في البيع بغير نص خلافاً لهما، وقوله: "لا" في أرضه عن أبي حنيفة روايتان في رواية "الأصل": لا يجب كما في داره؛ لأن من أجزاء الأرض، وليس في سائر الأجزاء منهما خمس، فكذا في هذا الجزء، وفي رواية "جامع الصغير": يجب؛ لأن الأرض لم تملك خالية عن المؤون بدليل وجوب العشر أو الخراج فيها بخلاف الدار. (فتح، مسكين)
Chapter of Rikaz: [Its definition: it is a name for what is under the earth by creation or buried by people. The first is called ma'din and the second kanz.] It is proper to mention this chapter in Siyar (biographies/Jihad) because what is taken from it is not zakat but is spent in the way of ghanimah (spoils), and they attached it to zakat because it is among financial duties, thus resembling zakat. He prefaced it to 'ushr because 'ushr is a burden with an element of worship, while rikaz is pure worship. It is kasr (broken) from razk meaning establishment, general for whether the establisher is the Creator or the created, so it is literal for ma'din and kanz is shared in meaning and not specific to buried treasure. (Fath) Khums: in the passive voice, lightened, i.e., taking one-fifth from a mine of naqd (gold/silver). You say: khamastu al-qawma akhmusuhum from the pattern nasara if you take one-fifth of their wealth. And a thing mukhammaş (with shadda) has five pillars as in al-Sihah, from which it is known that the shadda is not sound since there is no meaning to make it five-fifths only. The obligation of khums in the mine is due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'And in rikaz is one-fifth.' And it is called ma'din because it was in the hands of the disbelievers and our hands gained it by force, so it is ghanimah, and in ghanimah is one-fifth. (Fath) And the likes of iron: [i.e., also one-fifth for the likes of iron, and he intended by it every solid that is malleable by fire like lead and brass. (Fath)] He excludes liquids like tar, oil, and salt. Know that what is found under the ground is of two types: ma'din and kanz. There is no detail in kanz; khums is obligatory in it, whether it is of the same type as the earth or not, so long as it is valuable property, because it is the burial of disbelievers. Ma'din is of three types: a type that melts by fire and is malleable like gold, silver, and others; a type that does not melt nor is malleable like kohl and all stones; and a type that is liquid like tar and oil. Obligation is specific to the first type, not the others. And his saying: 'in kharaj or 'ushr land' means if something from these is found in kharaj or 'ushr land, then there is one-fifth. He excludes what is found in dar al-harb, for its land is not kharaj nor 'ushr land. Malik and al-Shafi'i say: Rikaz is not subject to khums because it is mubah (permissible) which one's hand preceded to, like firewood and similar, except if the extracted is gold or silver and reaches nisab, then zakat is obligatory, and no haul is required because it is for growth, resembling crops. Our evidence is what we narrated: 'And in rikaz is one-fifth.' So the khums is for the treasury (bayt al-mal), and four-fifths are for the finder. If he finds it in land owned by another, then four-fifths belong to the landowner and one-fifth to the treasury, whether the finder is free or slave, Muslim or dhimmi, adult or child, male or female. (Miskin, Fath) 'In kharaj land' means if he finds it in kharaji or 'ushri land, and the rest is for the finder. (T) 'Not in his house': i.e., khums is not taken from a mine of naqd or like iron found in his house, and the shop is like the house, because the mine is a dependent of the land, as evidenced by its inclusion in sale without specification, contrary to them (the two). His saying 'not in his land' from Abu Hanifa has two narrations: in the narration of al-Asl: it is not obligatory as in his house, because it is part of the earth, and in other parts there is no khums, so likewise in this part. In the narration of Jami' al-Saghir: it is obligatory, because the land is not owned free of burdens, as evidenced by the obligation of 'ushr or kharaj on it, unlike the house. (Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling of khums from mines (ma'din) and treasure (kanz). It begins by defining rikaz as anything found under the earth, either created (ma'din) or buried (kanz). The author explains that this chapter is placed under zakat but is actually about ghanimah (spoils of war), included here due to financial similarity. The core obligation is based on the Prophet's (ﷺ) hadith 'In rikaz is one-fifth.' Khums is only due on minerals that are malleable by fire (like gold, silver, iron, lead), not on liquids or non-malleable stones. The land in which it is found must be kharaj or 'ushr land (Muslim territory), not dar al-harb. An inter-madhhab dispute is noted: Malik and al-Shafi'i do not require khums on rikaz, considering it mubah property, only requiring zakat on gold/silver if nisab is reached without haul. The Hanafi proof is the hadith. Regarding ownership: if found on another's land, four-fifths go to the landowner and one-fifth to the treasury. If found in one's own house or land, there is disagreement within the Hanafi school: according to one narration from Abu Hanifa (in al-Asl), no khums; but according to another (in Jami' al-Saghir), khums is wajib. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that khums is wajib in one's own house/land, but the matn (Kanz) prefers the view of no khums in one's house.

Key Terms
ركاز (rikaz): Anything found under the earth, either natural (ma'din) or buried (kanz).
معدن (ma'din): A natural mineral deposit created in the earth (not buried by humans).
كنز (kanz): Treasure buried by humans (encompassing property left by disbelievers).
خمس (khums): One-fifth of the rikaz, paid to the Islamic treasury.
أرض خراج (ard kharaj): Land that is subject to kharaj (land tax) due to conquest or treaty.
أرض عشر (ard 'ushr): Land that is subject to 'ushr (tithe) because its owners accepted Islam or it's a peace treaty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a mine of gold found in one's own house?
A) Khums is obligatory according to all Hanafis.
B) Khums is not obligatory, based on one narration from Abu Hanifa, and this is the mufta bihi position.
C) Khums is not obligatory only if the house is in dar al-harb.
D) Zakat is obligatory instead of khums.

Key Takeaway
Khums on rikaz is wajib on malleable minerals found in kharaj or 'ushr land, but not in one's own house according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Ruling on Treasure (Kanz), Quicksilver, and Exemptions from Khums
Details ruling on buried treasure, quicksilver, and items exempt from khums

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when khums is wajib on treasure and quicksilver, identify the exceptions, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Khums is wajib on disbeliever treasure, not on pearls, amber, or non-malleable stones.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the rulings on buried treasure, quicksilver, and exemptions from khums. It clarifies distinctions based on stamps, land ownership, and mineral properties, with notable differences among the Hanafi imams. Understanding these nuances ensures correct application of khums obligations.

Guided Path
1
Classify the treasure by stamp
Determine if the treasure has Islamic markings (e.g., shahadah) or Jahili markings (e.g., idol). Islamic stamps make it luqata (lost item); Jahili stamps make it kanz subject to khums.
If stamp is ambiguous, default is Jahili in the apparent madhhab.
2
Apply ownership and location rules
If Kanzi is found in mubah land (no owner), the finder pays khums and keeps the rest. If found in owned land (e.g., one's house), khums is agreed, but the remainder goes to the mukhtat lahu (original grantee) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Abu Yusuf says remainder goes to the finder.
3
Analyze quicksilver (zibaq) via qiyas
Quicksilver is extracted by cooking stones, similar to metals like lead. Therefore, khums is wajib (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf likens it to tar/oil (exempt).
The majority view (the two) is mufta bihi.
4
Identify exempt items
Non-malleable stones (firuzaj, emeralds), pearls, amber, and all sea ornaments are exempt from khums. The hadith 'La khums fi al-hajar' (no khums on stone) supports this.
Abu Yusuf holds khums on sea items, but the school follows the majority.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi following the majority pays khums on quicksilver but not on pearls/amber. A follower of Abu Yusuf pays khums on both.
Tarjih: majority views are relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكنز، وباقيه للمختط له، وزيبق لا ركاز دار حرب، وفيروزج ولؤلؤ وعنبر. وكنز [بالرفع عطف على قوله: "معدن نقد" أي خمس كنز أيضاً وهو ما دفنه بنو آدم (ع)] اعلم أنه إذا وجد كنز فإن كان على ضرب أهل الإسلام كالمكتوب عليه كلمة الشهادة فهو لقطة، وحكمها: أنه يجب تعريفها، ثم التصدق على نفسه إن كان فقيراً أو على غيره إن كان غنياً، ولو كان على ضرب أهل الجاهلية كالمنقوش عليه الصنم، فإن وجده في أرض مباحة غير مملوكة لأحد ففيه الخمس وأربعة أخماسه للواجد، وإن وجده في دار نفسه أو أرضه ففيه الخمس اتفاقاً. (مسكين) وباقيه للمختط له وهو مختص بالصورة الواحدة، وهي إن وجده في دار نفسه أو أرضه لا مطلقاً، والمختط له : هو الذي ملكه الإمام هذه البقعة أول الفتح، وإنما سمي ! به؛ لأن الإمام يخط لكل واحد من الغانمين ناحية، ويقول: هذه لك، وإن لم يعرف المختط له أو ورثته صرف على أقصى مالك يعرف في الإسلام؛ لقيامه مقام صاحب الخط في هذه الدار، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف باقيه للواجد، ولو اشتبه الضرب بأن لم يكن فيه شيء من العلامات يجعل جاهلياً في ظاهر المذهب؛ لأنه الأصل، وقيل: إسلامياً في زماننا؛ لتقادم العهد. (مسكين، فتح) وزيبق بالياء وقد تهمز فارسي معرب، ولزوم الخمس فيه عند الطرفين خلافاً لأبي يوسف؛ لأنه ينطبع بنفسه وهو مائع ينبع من الأرض فأشبه القير والنفط ولهما أنه ينطبع مع غيره، فإنه حجر يطبخ، فيسيل منه الزيبق فأشبه الرصاص، والخلاف فيما وجد في معدنه، أما الموجود في خزائن الكفار ففيه الخمس اتفاقاً. فرع: الكنز سواء كان نقد أو غيره من السلاح والآلات وأثاث المنزل والمفصوص والقماش يجب فيها الخمس؛ لأنها كانت ملكاً للكفار ، فحوته أيدينا قهرا فصار غنيمة. (فتح) لا ركاز دار الحرب أي لا يخمس ركاز صحراء دار حرب وجده مستأمن فيها، فلو في بيتهم يرد عليهم. (ط) وفيروزج إلخ وكذا كل جامد لا ينطبع كالياقوت وسائر الجواهر إذا أخذت من معادنها. (ط)] أي لا يخمس فيروزج؛ لقوله عليه:" لا خمس في الحجر"، معطوف على قوله : "لا ركاز دار حرب"، وفيروزج معرب "فيروزه" وهو حجر مضيء يوجد في الجبال. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۲۷/۱۸] وقوله: "ولؤلؤ" هو مطر الربيع يقع في الصدف، فيصير لؤلؤاً، والصدف حيوان يخلق فيه اللؤلؤ، والعنبر حشيش نبت في البحر أو خثي دابة في البحر. [البحر الرائق: ۳۷۳/۲] وقال أبويوسف فيهما وفي كل حلية تخرج من البحر خمس؛ لأنه مما تحويه يد الملوك كالمعدن، ولهما: أن قعر البحر لا يرد عليه قهر أحد، فانعدمت اليد، وهي شرط لوجوب الخمس، ففي كل حلية تخرج من الذهب والفضة بأن كانتا كنزاً فيه لا يجب الخمس عند الطرفين. (فتح) ولؤلؤ : أي ولا يخمس أيضاً لؤلؤ ولا عنبر وكل حلية تستخرج من البحر. (ع)
And treasure [kanz], and its remainder is for the original grantee (mukhtat lahu), and quicksilver (zibaq), and no rikaz from dar al-harb, and firuzaj (turquoise), and pearls, and amber. And treasure [in the nominative, conjuncted to his saying 'a mine of naqd', i.e., also one-fifth for treasure, which is what the sons of Adam bury. (A) Know that when treasure is found, if it is of the stamp of the people of Islam, like the word of shahadah inscribed on it, then it is a lost item (luqata), and its ruling is that it must be announced, then given in charity by oneself if poor or by others if rich. If it is of the stamp of the people of Jahiliyyah, like an idol engraved on it, then if he finds it in mubah land not owned by anyone, it has one-fifth and four-fifths for the finder. If he finds it in his own house or land, it has one-fifth by agreement. (Miskin) Its remainder is for the mukhtat lahu, and this is specific to one scenario: when he finds it in his own house or land, not absolutely. The mukhtat lahu is the one to whom the Imam gave this tract at the time of the first conquest. He is called this because the Imam demarcates for each of the warriors a side and says: this is for you. If the mukhtat lahu or his heirs are unknown, it is given to the furthest known Muslim owner, because he stands in the place of the grantee of this house. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf says the remainder is for the finder. If the stamp is ambiguous, with no markings, it is considered Jahili in the apparent madhhab, because that is the original state. It is also said it is Islamic in our time due to the passage of time. (Miskin, Fath) And quicksilver (zibaq) with the ya' and sometimes hamza, a Persian arabicized word. The obligation of khums in it according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is contrary to Abu Yusuf, because it coalesces by itself and is a liquid that springs from the earth, resembling tar and oil. But according to the two, it coalesces with other things: it is a stone that is cooked, and quicksilver flows from it, so it resembles lead. The dispute is about what is found in its mine. As for what is found in the treasures of the disbelievers, khums is agreed upon. Branch: Treasure, whether it is cash or other like weapons, tools, household furniture, cut pieces, and cloth, khums is obligatory in them because they were the property of disbelievers and our hands took them by force, so they become ghanimah. (Fath) 'No rikaz from dar al-harb' means that rikaz from the desert of dar al-harb found by a musta'min (person under protection) is not subject to khums; if found in their homes, it is returned to them. (T) And firuzaj etc., likewise every solid that does not melt, like emeralds and all jewels, when taken from their mines. (T) i.e., firuzaj is not subject to khums due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No khums on stone.' It is conjoined to his saying 'no rikaz from dar al-harb'. Firuzaj is arabicized from 'firouzeh', a shining stone found in mountains. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 127/18] And his saying 'and pearls' is the spring rain that falls into the oyster shellfish and becomes pearls. The oyster is an animal in which pearls are created. Amber is a herb that grows in the sea or the dung of an animal in the sea. [Al-Bahr al-Raiq 373/2] Abu Yusuf said about them and about every ornament taken from the sea that there is khums, because it is what the kings possess, like a mine. But according to the two, the bottom of the sea is not dominated by force (qahr) of anyone, so possession (yad) is absent, and possession is a condition for the obligation of khums. Therefore, for every ornament taken from gold and silver, if they are treasure in the sea, khums is not obligatory according to the two. (Fath) And pearls: i.e., also pearls and amber and every ornament extracted from the sea are not subject to khums. (A)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings on buried treasure (kanz) and various exceptions to khums. It begins by distinguishing treasure with Islamic markings (luqata, requiring announcement and charity) from pre-Islamic treasure (requires khums). If found in mubah land, the finder gets four-fifths; if found in one's own house/land, khums is agreed. The remainder after khums for treasure in owned land goes to the original grantee (mukhtat lahu) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf says it goes to the finder. If the stamp is ambiguous, the default is Jahili (pre-Islamic) in the Hanafi madhhab, though some say Islamic in later times. Then a dispute on quicksilver (zibaq): Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider it akin to malleable metals (like lead) and thus subject to khums, while Abu Yusuf likens it to liquid minerals like tar, which are exempt. The text clarifies that any treasure from disbelievers (cash, weapons, etc.) has khums. Exceptions: no khums on rikaz from dar al-harb (if found by a musta'min) nor on non-malleable stones like firuzaj (turquoise), pearls, and amber, based on the hadith 'No khums on stone.' Abu Yusuf dissents on sea items, but the school position is no khums.

Key Terms
كنز (kanz): Buried treasure from human beings; classified by stamp (Islamic vs Jahili) for ruling.
لقطة (luqata): Lost item; treasure with Islamic markings is treated as luqata, requiring announcement and charity.
مختط له (mukhtat lahu): The original grantee of a land tract given by the Imam after conquest.
زيبق (zibaq): Quicksilver (mercury), a mineral that is liquid but extracted from cooked stones.
فيروزج (firuzaj): Turquoise or similar precious stones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on pearls extracted from the sea?
A) Khums is wajib according to all Hanafi imams.
B) Khums is not wajib according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the majority).
C) Khums is wajib only if the pearls are found in a mine on land.
D) Zakat is wajib instead of khums.

Key Takeaway
Khums is wajib on treasure from disbelievers but not on non-malleable stones, pearls, amber, or sea ornaments; quicksilver is subject to khums according to the majority Hanafi view.
