Rulings on Treasure Trove and Khums
Ruling on treasure trove with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for khums on treasure trove, distinguish between pre-Islamic and Islamic coinage, and apply the majority and minority positions on mercury, pearls, and ambergris.

Core Idea
Khums on treasure trove is wajib, but the remainder may go to the allottee or finder depending on the land.

Treasure trove (rikaz) refers to pre-Islamic buried wealth. The Hanafi school imposes khums (one-fifth) on such treasure, with the remainder distributed based on land ownership. This lesson unpacks the key rulings, including the treatment of mercury, pearls, and ambergris, and highlights the well-known intra-Hanafi disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Classify the Treasure
Determine if the treasure has Islamic or pre-Islamic markings. Islamic markings (e.g., shahada) make it luqata (lost property); pre-Islamic markings make it rikaz subject to khums.
Ambiguous markings default to pre-Islamic in the madhhab.
2
Identify the Land Type
If found in ownerless land, the finder gets four-fifths after khums. If found in owned land, khums is due by consensus, but the remainder goes to the allottee (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) or the finder (Abu Yusuf).
The allottee is the original recipient of the land from the Imam.
3
Apply the Ruling on Mercury
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule khums wajib on mercury because it resembles lead (a smelted mineral). Abu Yusuf disagrees, comparing it to naturally flowing oil.
This disagreement applies only to mercury found in its mine; in disbelievers' treasuries, khums is agreed.
4
Exempt Stones and Sea Items
Turquoise and similar stones are exempt based on the hadith 'No khums on stone.' Pearls and ambergris are also exempt (majority view) because the sea is not subject to forceful ownership.
Abu Yusuf holds khums wajib on sea items, analogizing them to mines.
5
Derive the Practical Consequence
The khilaf has practical effect: if khums is not wajib, the finder keeps all; if it is, one-fifth is paid. The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Gold and silver items from the sea are exempt according to both parties if they are treasure.

كتاب الزكاة ۲۰۸ باب الركاز س وكنز، وباقيه للمختط له، وزيبق لا ركاز دار حرب، وفيروزج ولؤلؤ وعنبر. وكنز [بالرفع عطف على قوله: "معدن نقد" أي خمس كنز أيضاً وهو ما دفنه بنو آدم (ع)] اعلم أنه إذا وجد كنز فإن كان على ضرب أهل الإسلام كالمكتوب عليه كلمة الشهادة فهو لقطة، وحكمها: أنه يجب تعريفها، ثم التصدق على نفسه إن كان فقيراً أو على غيره إن كان غنياً، ولو كان على ضرب أهل الجاهلية كالمنقوش عليه الصنم، فإن وجده في أرض مباحة غير مملوكة لأحد ففيه الخمس وأربعة أخماسه للواجد، وإن وجده في دار نفسه أو أرضه ففيه الخمس اتفاقاً. Book of Zakat. Page 208. Chapter of Rikaz. And a treasure, and its remainder is for the allottee, and mercury is not rikaz in dar al-harb, and turquoise and pearl and ambergris. And treasure (in nominative as coordinated with his saying: "mine of coin") meaning: also the khums of a treasure trove, and it is what the sons of Adam buried. Know that when a treasure is found, if it is of the coinage of the people of Islam, like having the word of testimony inscribed on it, then it is a luqata (lost and found), and its ruling is that it must be announced, then given in charity from oneself if poor, or from others if rich. And if it is of the coinage of the people of ignorance, like having an idol engraved on it, then if he finds it in a vacant land not owned by anyone, then khums (one-fifth) applies and four-fifths are for the finder. If he finds it in his own house or land, then khums applies by agreement. (Miskin) And the remainder is for the allottee, and this is specific to one scenario: that he finds it in his own house or land, not absolutely. The allottee is the one to whom the Imam gave this area at the time of first conquest; he is so named because the Imam draws a line for each of the spoils-takers and says: this is for you. If the allottee or his heirs are not known, it is spent for the farthest known owner in Islam, because he stands in the place of the line-holder in this house. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: the remainder is for the finder. According to them, it flows with others; it is a stone that is cooked and from which mercury flows, resembling lead. The disagreement is regarding what is found in its mine. Sub-branch: Treasure, whether coin or other things like weapons, tools, household furnishings, cut gems, and cloth, khums applies in them because they were the property of disbelievers and our hands enveloped them by force, so they became spoils. Turquoise is Persianized from "firuzah", it is a luminous stone found in mountains. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 127/18] And his saying: "And pearl": it is the spring rain that falls into the oyster shell and becomes pearl; the oyster is a living creature in which pearl is created. Ambergris is a herb that grew in the sea or the dung of a sea animal. [Al-Bahr al-Raiq: 373/2] Abu Yusuf said regarding both and every ornament extracted from the sea that khums applies because it is what the hands of kings encompass like a mine. According to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): the bottom of the sea is not subject to forceful taking by anyone, so the hand (ownership) is absent, and hand is a condition for the obligation of khums. So regarding every ornament extracted from gold and silver if they were treasure in it, khums does not apply according to both parties. Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the rulings on treasure trove (rikaz) and related items. The type is a ruling with proof, with intra-Hanafi disputes. The author first defines 'kanz' (treasure) as what is buried by humans. If the treasure bears Islamic markings (e.g., the shahada), it is treated as luqata (lost property) and must be announced and given in charity. If it bears pre-Islamic markings (e.g., an idol), then if found in ownerless land, one-fifth (khums) is due and four-fifths go to the finder. If found in one's own property, khums is due by consensus. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the remainder (after khums) goes to the allottee (mukhtatt lahu) — the original grantee of the land by the Imam — while Abu Yusuf says it goes to the finder. If markings are ambiguous, it defaults to pre-Islamic in the madhhab, but some say Islamic in later times. Regarding mercury (zibaq): Abu Hanifa and Muhammad impose khums on it because it is a mineral that can be melted (like lead), while Abu Yusuf exempts it because it is a liquid that flows naturally (like oil). However, if found in the treasuries of disbelievers, khums is agreed upon. Any treasure trove of disbelievers' property is subject to khums regardless of type. Next, for dar al-harb: a musta'min finding rikaz in the desert does not pay khums; if in a house, it is returned to the owners. Turquoise (firuzaj) and similar stones are exempt from khums based on the hadith 'No khums on stone.' Pearls (lu'lu') and ambergris ('anbar) from the sea are not subject to khums, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because the sea is not subject to forceful ownership. Abu Yusuf disagrees for items that kings collect. The relied-upon position is the majority, except for gold and silver items if they are found as treasure in the sea.

Key Terms
كنز (kanz): Treasure trove: wealth buried by humans (not natural minerals).
ركاز (rikaz): Buried treasure of pre-Islamic origin, or sometimes used synonymously with ma'din (mine) but here it means treasure trove.
مختط له (mukhtatt lahu): The person to whom the Imam allotted a plot of land (by drawing a line) during the first conquest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling on mercury (zibaq) found in its mine?
A) Khums is not wajib because it is a liquid like oil.
B) Khums is wajib because it is obtained by smelting stone like lead.
C) Khums is wajib only if the mercury value exceeds 200 dirhams.
D) Khums is not wajib under any circumstance.

Key Takeaway
Khums on pre-Islamic treasure is wajib, but the school's key controversies involve the remainder distribution, mercury, and sea items—always check the condition of forceful hand (yad).

Obligation of 'Ushr on Honey and Perishable Produce: The Hanafi Position Without Nisab
Ruling on 'ushr (tithe) for honey, rain-fed crops, and the nisab condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'ushr for honey and vegetables, explain the nisab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: 'Ushr is wajib on all produce, including honey and vegetables, without nisab.

The obligation of 'ushr (tithe) on agricultural produce is a key aspect of Hanafi fiqh. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that, according to Abu Hanifa, 'ushr is due on every type of produce from 'ushr land, including honey and perishable vegetables, without requiring a minimum amount (nisab) or that the produce last a year. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagreed, imposing nisab and persistence conditions. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying 'ushr rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening lines: 'ushr is wajib on honey from 'ushr land and on rain-fed and flow-irrigated crops, without condition of nisab or persistence.
This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Find where the text mentions the Sahibayn's conditions: Abu Yusuf requires five awsuq (value) for nisab; Muhammad requires five afraq for honey.
They also require persistence for vegetables.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa's evidence: Quran 2:267 (general command) and hadith 'in ma saqat al-sama' al-'ushr'. The Sahibayn's proofs: hadiths about nisab and 'no sadaqah on vegetables'.
The text interprets opposing hadiths as referring to zakat al-tijarah or official tax.
4
Apply the dispute to a practical case
Consider a farmer harvesting 2 kg of honey and 1 kg of parsley. According to Abu Hanifa, 'ushr is due on both. According to the Sahibayn, no 'ushr because nisab not met.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

[رمز الحقائق: ۱۲۸/۱] ومسقي سماء: أي ويجب أيضاً في كل شيء سقي بمطر، سمي المطر سماء مجازاً من قبيل ذكر الشيء باسم ما يجاوره أو ما يحل فيه. (ع) وسيح أي مسقي سيح، وهو ماء أنهار وأودية. Chapter of 'Ushr. Chapter of 'Ushr. T.S. i.e. in explanation of its rulings. It is obligatory on honey from 'ushr land, and on what is watered by rain and by flowing water, without the condition of nisab and persistence except... i.e. the 'ushr. S M F S M F i.e. in all. i.e. waterwheel. Firewood, sugarcane, and grass. And half of it (half 'ushr) on what is watered by a bucket or a waterwheel, i.e. the Persian. i.e. half 'ushr is obligatory. The great bucket. 6 And according to Ahmad. Chapter of 'Ushr: He intended by 'ushr what is attributed to it to include half 'ushr and double it, and he mentioned it in Zakat because it is disbursed to its recipients. It is obligatory on the land of a minor, an insane person, waqf land, the authorized (ma'dhun), and the mukatab, and without the passing of a year, such that if the land produces multiple times, it is obligatory each time. Its name is Zakat metaphorically. Its pillar is ownership (tamlik), its cause is the land that grows produce in reality. Conditions of payment are like Zakat, and it is cancelled by its destruction or destruction of part proportionally. (Fath) 'On honey': i.e. found in 'ushr land, not kharaj land. (T, Ayn) 'Ushr land: if he said: 'honey from non-kharaj land' it would be better to include wilderness and mountains, because 'ushr is obligatory according to both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf because it is a cancellation, and it is a sale in the case of others. Al-Shafi'i and Malik said: it is not obligatory because it is produced from an animal. Our evidence: the transmission (athar) to that effect, 'ushr is obligatory on it even if from kharaj land. Then according to Abu Hanifa, it is obligatory whether it reaches nisab or not. According to Abu Yusuf, if its value reaches five awsuq and one wasq is sixty sa' then 'ushr applies; in a narration, five amnad (man). According to Muhammad, if it reaches five afraq (plural of faraq) it is obligatory, and a faraq (with two fathas) is thirty-six ratls Iraqi. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 128/1] 'And (what is) watered by sky': i.e. it is also obligatory on everything watered by rain; rain is called sky metaphorically, as mentioning something by its container. (Ayn) 'And flowing water' meaning watered by flowing water (saih) which is water of rivers and valleys. (T) 'And persistence' [i.e. and without condition of persistence in what is watered by sky or flowing water, so it is obligatory on vegetables that do not last. (T)] This is according to Abu Hanifa, for with him 'ushr is obligatory on everything the earth produces without requiring nisab or that it lasts a year, so it applies to vegetables and greens. They (the two, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not obligatory except for what has lasting fruit, due to his statement (upon him): 'There is no sadaqah on grain or fruit until it reaches five awsuq.' And his statement: 'There is no sadaqah on vegetables.' And the three (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad?) said that. And his (Abu Hanifa's) evidence is the generality of His statement: 'Spend of the good things you have earned and of what We have brought forth for you from the earth' (2:267) and his statement: 'On what is watered by the sky and clouds, 'ushr, and on what is watered by a bucket, half 'ushr', related by Muslim and others. What they narrated is interpreted as zakat of trade because they used to trade by awsuq, and a wasq at that time was forty dirhams. The second hadith, if authentic, is taken to refer to sadaqah collected by the tax collector ('ashir). [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 128/1] 'And grass if he did not make his land (intended) for these things.' (T) An exception from his statement: 'the 'ushr is obligatory' because these things are not intended for land exploitation, like palm branches, figs, seeds of watermelon, cucumber, gum, and tar. What is meant by sugarcane (qasab) is Persian sugarcane from which pens are made. As for sugar cane and medicinal cane (qasab al-zarirah) which is ground and used in medicine, 'ushr applies. And if he makes his land a plantation of sugarcane, or firewood, or grass, 'ushr is obligatory. (Fath)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the obligation of 'ushr (tithe) on agricultural produce, specifically honey and crops. The type is a ruling with proof, involving intra-Hanafi disputes and inter-madhhab differences. The author first states that 'ushr is wajib on honey from 'ushr land and on crops watered by rain or flowing water, without requiring nisab or the produce lasting (i.e., perishable items are included). This is the position of Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require nisab and that the produce be of lasting quantity. Abu Hanifa's proof is the general Quranic verse (2:267) and the hadith 'On what is watered by the sky, 'ushr; on what is watered by a bucket, half 'ushr.' He interprets the contradictory hadiths about nisab as referring to zakat of trade or to the 'ushr collected by the tax collector. There is also a dispute on honey: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say 'ushr is wajib even without nisab; Abu Yusuf requires nisab of five awsuq; Muhammad requires five afraq. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools say no zakat on honey because it is from an animal, but the Hanafi response is that there is transmitted evidence for 'ushr on honey. Exceptions to 'ushr include items not intended for land exploitation, such as firewood, grass (if not planted), palm branches, figs, seeds, gum, etc. However, if one plants such items intentionally, 'ushr becomes wajib. The chapter also notes that 'ushr applies to land of minors, waqf, etc., and is due each time the land produces, without yearly cycle.

Key Terms
عشر (ushr): The tithe (one-tenth) payable on agricultural produce and honey from 'ushr land.
وسق (wasq): A unit of dry measure equal to 60 sa' (approx. 120-160 kg) used for nisab of produce.
فرق (faraq): A measure equal to 36 ratls (Iraqi), used by Muhammad as the nisab for honey.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what is the nisab requirement for 'ushr on vegetables?
A) Nisab of 5 awsuq is required.
B) No nisab is required; 'ushr is due on any amount.
C) Nisab of 5 afraq is required.
D) Nisab of 200 dirhams is required.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's position, that 'ushr is wajib on all produce of 'ushr land without nisab and including honey and vegetables, is the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

Expenses Not Deducted from 'Ushr
States that cultivation expenses are not deducted from the tithe

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that cultivation expenses are not deducted from 'ushr and explain its practical application.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'ushr is due on gross produce — no deduction for expenses.

This lesson examines a specific rule regarding zakat on agricultural produce. The Hanafi school holds that expenses incurred in cultivation, such as wages and animal feed, are not deductible from the total yield. This distinguishes Hanafi fiqh from other schools that allow some deductions. Understanding this rule clarifies the basis of 'ushr obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? It is the obligation of 'ushr (tithe) on agricultural produce. The ruling is that the tithe is obligatory on the entire produce.
2
Understand the term 'mu'un'
The text specifies that 'al-mu'un' (expenses) are not deducted. This includes wages for workers and feed for oxen — essentially all direct cultivation costs.
3
Apply the ruling to the gross produce
Since expenses are not deducted, the tithe is calculated on the gross output, not net profit. Any produce that reaches the threshold (nisab) is subject to 'ushr in full.
4
Compare with other schools
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school. Other madhahib (e.g., Shafi'i) allow deduction of expenses under certain conditions, impacting the amount of zakah due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ترفع المؤون. المؤون: جمع مؤونة كأجرة العمال ونفقة البقر، أي لا تحتسب ولا تستثنى هذه الأشياء بل يجب العشر في كل الخارج.
And expenses are not deducted. Al-mu'un: plural of ma'unah, like wages of workers and expenses of oxen, meaning these things are not counted and not exempted, rather the tithe is obligatory on the entire produce.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explained in its commentary, regarding the zakah on agricultural produce ('ushr). The text asserts that expenses (mu'un) incurred in cultivation, such as wages for laborers and feed for oxen, are not deducted from the total harvest. The tithe is due on the gross produce, not the net profit. This is a unique Hanafi position differing from some other schools that allow deduction of expenses. The commentary defines mu'un to clarify the scope of what is not deductible.

Key Terms
المؤون (al-mu'un): Plural of ma'unah, meaning expenses of cultivation such as wages and animal feed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, are expenses of cultivation deducted from the total produce for calculating 'ushr?
A) Yes, all expenses are deducted.
B) No, expenses are not deducted; 'ushr is due on the entire produce.
C) Only wages are deducted, not animal feed.
D) Only animal feed is deducted, not wages.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the obligation of 'ushr is on the gross produce, and no expenses of cultivation are deductible.
